# Introduction
## Background 

The electronic patient record has not yet fully replaced the paper-based one. Rather, electronic documentation usually is used in addition to paper-based records. One might assume that the electronic data represent a subset of the patient data stored in the paper-based record. However, Mikkelsen and Aasly cited by Stausberg et al. (2003) found that parallel use of electronic and paper-based patient records result[ed] in inconsistencies between the record systems and documentation was missing in both. The paper-based patient record is still the main source for information management in daily care delivery for several reasons. Utilization of the paper-based patient record, both as a reminder to health care providers to report events, such as the course of an illness, and as a tool for communication among clinicians (@Stausberg2003).

Since the rapid scale-up of antiretroviral therapy (ART) programs, electronic patient tracking systems (EPTS) have been deployed to respond to the growing demand for program monitoring, evaluation and reporting to governments and donors. These routinely collected data are often used in epidemiologic and operations research studies intended to improve programs. To ensure accurate reporting and good quality for research, the reliability and completeness of data systems need to be assessed and reported (@Lambdin2012). It was assessed the completeness and reliability of ePTS, used in health facilities implemented by partners on HIV care and treatment health facilities across all provinces of Mozambique.

## Objectives 

1. Evaluate the agreement of the existing data in the patient files and the data in the electronic patient tracking system (ePTS);
2. Evaluate the completeness of the data entered into the electronic database;
3. Improve the quality of data in ePTS.










# Methodology 
## Electronic Patient Tracking System Data Quality Assessments (ePTS DQA)

The data quality assessment on electronic patient tracking system, was performed shortly before the last round of SAPR 2018 assessment period, with a sample of patient files to evaluate the completeness and reliability of key variables from patient charts with the information stored into electronic system for patients receiving HIV treatment enrolled in health facilities. The data from the electronic database was compared with sample of data from patient charts. 

Each health facility with ePTS compares selected variables (see table 2 below) in patient charts with the information stored in the database to measure concordance. For the health facility conducting the first ePTS DQA, reviews a representative sample of patient charts active on ART. For health facilities with subsequent ePTS DQA, engages a convenience sample across select sub-populations (i.e. pediatric patients, TB con-infected patients, ANC patients etc.). In contrast to the iDQA and eDQA activities, where the focus is on improving aggregate data in the national health information system, this activity focuses on individual-level patient monitoring systems. Target concordance between patient charts and ePTS is 90% to be considered as good quality of data. 

## Patient Chart Selection for ePTS DQA 
### Representative Sample for first round of ePTS DQA

<figure>
<img style="float: left; " src="images/popsize.jpg">
<figcaption><a>Table 1: Representative Sample</a></figcaption>
</figure>
<br/>
1. Determine the sample size using Table 1, based on the total number of patients active on ART during the review period. <br/>
2. Determine the interval size to select a randomized sample of patient charts. Divide the total number of patients active on ART who had at least one consultation during the review period of three months, by the total number of patients in the sample. For example, if there were 43022 patients active on ART with consult and 146 is the sample size, the interval would be 30. 4322/146 = 30<br/>
3. Determine the beginning point for sample selection. Take the interval of 30 and randomly select a number that falls within the interval. For example, if the interval is 1- 30 and the randomly selected number is 8, that will be the first patient chart selected from the total population. <br/>
4. Continue to select starting number plus interval (for example starting point for sample of 30 is the 8th patient on the list, the second is the 38th on the list, the thired is number 68 etc. until the total sample has been selected. If when you go to pull the chart and can’t be located, take the immediate next one. For example, if you cannot locate the chart that is number 38 on the list of all patients active on ART who received a consult during the review period, select the next chart, 39. 
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>
<br/>

### Convenience Sample for Sub-Sequent ePTS-DQA 

After the first ePTS DQA in a facility, the sub-sequent DQA or follow-up DQA use a convenience sample of 50 charts, from select sub-populations of patients, as follows: 

* 10 patients who received ART during a pre-natal consult (ANC)
* 20 adult patients active on ART 
*	10 patients from the ART Aderence Community Groups  
*	5 pediatric patients on ART
*	5 patients active on ART and also receiving TB treatment 

For each sub-population, pull the total sample of patients active on ART. Using the sample size as the interval, select the convenience sample. For example, query the total list of patients who received ART during ANC care during the review period. Pull the chart of the $10^{th}$ patient on the list and continue to pull every $10^{th}$ chart until you have the total sample of 10 charts. 

## Electronic Patient Tracking System DQA Indicators
Electronic patient tracking system DQA assesses the quality of variables (see table 2) in the database, compared with the information in the clinical files (primary source of the data).


![**Table 2**: Variables assessed during the ePTS DQA activities](images/tabela1.jpg)

## eEPTS DQA Data Collection 

Using an Excel book (not shown here), teams pull the charts identified in the patent sampling and compare the data in the charts with the data in the ePTS. Possible results are: 

a)	**X**: Errors in documentation in patient file: No data in the patient file 
b)	**0**: Concordance: Data in the patent file and the data in the ePTS match 
c)	**1**: Missing Data Entry: Data in the patient file has not been entered into the ePTS
d)	**2**: Error in Data Entry: Data in the patient file and data in the ePTS do not match 
e)	Error in documentation: data are in the ePTS but not in the patient file (similar to X)
    i.	**3a)** Possible to confirm with another primary source document
    ii.	**3b)** Data entry error: Not possible to confirm with another primary source document
f)	Not Applicable: When the data are not applicable, such as pregnancy for men 


## ePTS DQA Data Analysis 
### Calculating the Concordance 

The concordance divides the absolute value counted in the database by the information found in the clinical file. Concordance is defined by two conditions: 

1. Data in the patient chart matches data in the ePTS database (i.e. variable present and is the same value)
2. Data in the patient chart is missing, but it can be confirmed and matched via an alternate primary source, such as pharmacy receipts. 

For ePTS DQA the goal is to achieve a 90% concordance with the clinical file data (patient file). If the objective is not reached, actions plans have to be put in place in order to improve the quality of the data.

**Concordance:**
<div class="alert alert-block alert-info">
$$
  \frac{ABS(\mbox{data recounted in EPTS database})}{\mbox{Data recounted in the clinical file or confirmed in an alternate primary document}}
$$
</div>

It is expected to reach a minimum agreement of 90%. The US with less than 90% agreement is strongly recommended to enhance typing and mentoring for the correct completion of clinical processes.






# Key Findings 
## Number of health facilities assessed {#fig1}
An overall of 278 health facilities conducted the data quality assessment in ePTS, whereby the majority of health facilities were assessed in Maputo province (58), followed by Zambezia (47), Gaza (32), Maputo City (27) and 26 in Inhambane province. Tete, Manica, Sofala and Niassa assessed less than 20 health facilities by each province with Niassa and Sofala provinces only assessed 5 and 7 health facilities respectively. [Figure 1](#fig1) shows the interactive maps with clustered number of health facilities assessed together with the volume of patients currently on ART [^1] by facility.

[^1]: Data of volume of patients in ART shared by HIV program.

## Number of patient charts selected

We looked at the number of health facilities selected by province, with the mean of the median number of patient charts selected in each health facility including the minimum and maximum number of patient files aggregated by province. Maputo City, Manica and Gaza, they have selected a sample of patients files greater than 50, which we can realize that it may be possible that some of the health facilities in those provinces, they conducted first round of ePTS DQA (Table 3).

### Sample of patient charts selected by site {#hfsample}

Two health facilities in Maputo City, HG José Macamo and CS Chamanculo had selected a sample of 100 and 150 patients files selected for assessment respectively; and in Manica province, CS Inchope had selected a sample of 146 patient files and in Gaza province two health facilities (CS Zongoene and CS Manjangue) had selected a sample of 100 and 146 patient files selected respectively. There were some health facilities with a sample of patient files less than 50 and out of scope to be considered as part of the first round of assessment or convenience sample for sub-sequent assessment. More details on sample of patient chart per health facility can be seen in Table 4 below.

### Volume of patientes on ART across the sites assessed {#volume}

Regarding the volume of sites assessed, [Figure 2](#volume) highlights some trends, where in north region we see the majority of health facilities had medium to low volume of patients, medium to high volume in central region and a mixture of all level of volume of patientes for the sites assessed in south regoin, mainly in Gaza, Maputo City and Maputo province.

## Global Concordance assessed {#conc_global}

We derived the values which corresponds to the global concordance, as the result of the mean of the concordance from the primary souce of variables used to assess this activity whitin each health facility. From [Figure 3](#conc_global), it seems that the majority of health facilities had concordance of good quality, i.e, the patient files selected for the assessment matched with the electronic information into the database. There were some health facilities clustered in Gaza and Tete province with global concordance less than the required by the protocol of 90%, meaning that the information from electronic patient system differ from original source. We also see this pattern on three health facilities in Nampula and Zambezia and one health facility in Sofala and Inhambane province with concordance showing bad quality of data into the electronic systems.

### Concordance versus SAPR 2018 deviations{#deviation}

We also looked at the global concordance on each health facility with the deviations from the last round of assessment in SAPR 2018 period with the volume of patient currently on ART. By definition, the assessment of concordance happens before the iDQA, to decide if the health facility it's going to use electronic system during the iDQA round. From the iDQA SAPR 2018 we selected three indicators which matches with those defined by the ePTS protocol for convenience sample for sub-sequent ePTS assessment: ART, ANC and pediatric. The remain indicators doesn't match with those defined by ePTS protocol. 

Therefore, from [Figure 4](#deviation), highlights a trend of two scenarios: First, the majority of health facilities had concordance greater than 90% which means that the more than 90% of patient charts selected matched with data into electronic system. There is some differences in terms of volume of patients in all of the indicator with most of health facilities selected for SAPR 2018 with low volume of patients on ART. Second, as most of health facilities assessed in the last round of iDQA SAPR 2018 had low volume of patients, half of those health facilities had deviations less than 10% (green dashed) and around one third of health facilities had deviations greater than 20% (red dashed), meaning that those facilities with good concordance had deviations with poor quality in terms of reporting at the health facility. There are three health facilities with outstanding outliers in deviations, two of them good concordance.

![Figure 4: Scatterplot chart of global concordance and SAPR 2018 deviation
 by volume of patients](index_files/figure-html/global-1.png)

### Distribution of data quality issues {#issues}

We assessed the most outstanding issues related with data quality on electronic systems: data entry errors, documentation error and the lack of data entry. We mapped those issues from all health facilities as shown in [Figure 5](#issues). It highlights that the majority of health facilities across the country had problems related with lack of data entry possibly from the staff assigned to enter data into the electronic system and documentation error possibly from clinical staff were not registering information into the patient files. There were some few health facilities with issues related with data entry errors.

### Quality issues from each variables {#var}

We analyzed each specific issue in the assessment of ePTS related to the documentation error, data entry error and lack of data entry, looking specifically at the variables from the primary sources of data used for concordance assessment. For the case of documentation error, it was verified that more than 20% of documentation errors were more related to laboratory area, by not being able to register the date of sample collection, result and date of delivering the viral load results, as well as the PMTCT area for not being able to register the fields related to date probably for delivery, date of last menstruation, pregnancy in ART and so on. Another area with major issue was GAAC where more than 65% of patient files did not have information regarding this [Figure 6.1](#var). The same was verified for cases of lack of data entry into the system for the PMTCT and TB areas that were above 15% (see [Figure 6.2](#var)). For the case of data entry error in the system, this was below 9% in most of the variables assessed ( [Figure 6.3](#var)).

 from the primary source](index_files/figure-html/errordoc-1.png)


![Figure 6.2: Percentage of lack of data entry of each variable
 from the primary source](index_files/figure-html/errorlack-1.png)


![Figure 6.3: Percentage of data entry error of each variable
 from the primary source](index_files/figure-html/errordig-1.png)

# Discussion

The scale-up of patient on antiretroviral therapy and deployment of electronic patient tracking systems it's the major goal to respond to the growing demand for program monitoring, evaluation and reporting to governments and donors. Results from the last round of data quality assessments on electronic patient tracking system showed a range of good quality data across health facilities and provinces, meaning that the majority of health facilities assessed there was concordance greater than 90% from data entered into the electronic system with data from patient charts. But there were some facilities mainly in Gaza and Tete provinces which highlighted some problems in concordance of data from electronic system and patient charts. A paper-based patient record should be a gold standard representing the truth regarding the patient as the reference point for the comparison with the medical records types.

The ART, ANC and PCR were the indicator assessed from the deviations of the last round of assessment of SAPR 2018 period, along with the volume of patients current on ART. Therefore, the majority of health facilities had concordance greater than 90% which means that the more than 90% of patient chart selected matched with data into electronic system. Most of sites selected for iDQA had low volume of patients, where half of those facilities had deviations less than 10% and around one third of facilities had deviations greater than 20%, which highlights that one third of facilities with good concordance assessed as the first step, had poor quality in terms of quality of data reported from the health facility. There were some health facilities with outstanding outliers in deviations, two of them good concordance.

Regarding to the issues of concordance, it was found that the majority of health facilities across the country had problems related with lack of data entry and documentation error. The ack of data entry could be due to the staff assigned to enter data into the electronic system, possibly because of less concentration during the data entry process and documentation error could be linked in most of the cases to the providers staff not registering all information required into patient files. Also, we found some health health facilities with issues related with typos during the data entry process. Most of the issues of concordance related to documentation and lack of data entry were more concentrated in health facilities in the south region, while issues related with data entry error and lack of data entry were more concentrated in center and north region of the country.


# Conclusion

The lessons learned from this assessment were that professionals staff and providers should be cognizant of the possible discrepancies between paper and electronic information and look toward combining information from both records whenever appropriate. Most of health facilities still have the agreement of the existing data in the patient files and the data in the electronic patient tracking system, though, some health facilities in two provinces still poses some challenges on data stored in electronic systems, when compared with paper-based records.  

Quality issues related with documentation of information into the patient files and lack of data entry into the system, where the major issues found on this evaluation. This was highlighted in areas related to laboratoty area of not being able to register the date of sample collection, result and date of delivering the viral load results, as well as the PMTCT area for not being able to register the fields related to date probably for delivery, date of last menstruation, pregnancy in ART.
